# Chapter 24 — Packaging Strategy

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches how to package offers so they are easier to sell.

A good package makes the decision feel clear.

A bad package makes the client feel like they are buying a pile of services.

---

## The three package levels

For most verticals, package offers into three levels:

1. **Review**
2. **Starter System**
3. **Growth System**

This keeps the buyer from being overwhelmed.

---

## Package 1 — Review

Best for:

- curious owners
- unclear needs
- skeptical buyers
- early conversations
- multiple possible problems
- businesses not ready for full build

Example names:

- AI Growth Review
- AI Opportunity Review
- Business AI Readiness Review
- AI First Move Review

Recommended pricing:

$497 – $1,500

Simple explanation:

> We review the business and identify the first useful AI move.

---

## Package 2 — Starter System

Best for:

- one clear problem
- one first useful system
- smaller budget
- faster launch
- lower risk
- first client relationship

Example names:

- AI Website + Conversion Starter
- AI Content Engine Starter
- Smart Intake Starter
- AI Customer Answer Starter
- Internal Assistant Starter
- Paid Campaign + Landing Page Starter

Recommended pricing:

$1,500 – $8,000 setup  
$250 – $2,000/month if ongoing

Simple explanation:

> We build the first useful system around one clear business problem.

---

## Package 3 — Growth System

Best for:

- multiple connected problems
- businesses ready for a serious engagement
- clients who want ongoing support
- higher-value businesses
- multi-location or complex businesses

Example names:

- Full AI Growth System
- AI Business Growth System
- AI Adoption + Growth System
- AI Advantage System

Recommended pricing:

$7,500 – $35,000+ setup  
$1,500 – $7,500+/month

Simple explanation:

> We connect marketing, customer answers, intake, automation, internal assistants, and ongoing AI improvement.

---

## Vertical packaging examples

### Home Services + Contractors

Review:
- Home Services AI Growth Review

Starter:
- Quote Request + Photo Intake System
- Missed Call + Follow-Up System
- Service Area Content Starter
- Internal Estimate/Proposal Assistant

Growth:
- Full Contractor AI Growth System

---

### Restaurants

Review:
- Restaurant AI Growth Review

Starter:
- Private Events + Catering Intake
- Guest Questions + Concierge Planning
- QR/Menu Path System
- Short-Form Content + Local Visibility Starter

Growth:
- Restaurant AI Growth System
- Restaurant Partner Program + AI Growth System

---

### Hospitality + Local Experience

Review:
- Hospitality AI Growth Review

Starter:
- Event Inquiry System
- Guest Questions Operator
- Local Experience Content Engine
- QR Campaign Path

Growth:
- Hospitality AI Growth System

---

### Professional Services

Review:
- Professional Services AI Growth Review

Starter:
- Consultation Intake + Qualification
- Authority Content + AI Search Visibility
- Approved FAQ / Process Answer Library
- Internal Research + Proposal Assistant

Growth:
- Professional Services AI Growth System

---

### Transportation + Concierge

Review:
- Transportation + Concierge AI Growth Review

Starter:
- Route Request Intake
- Provider Handoff Workflow
- Group Pickup Request System
- Route Page + Local SEO Starter

Growth:
- Transportation + Concierge AI Growth System

---

### Local Brands + Retail

Review:
- Local Brand AI Growth Review

Starter:
- Product Question Answer Library
- QR Campaign System
- Local Content Engine
- Promotion Landing Page

Growth:
- Local Brand AI Growth System

---

## The starter-first rule

When uncertain, sell the starter first.

A starter system:

- lowers risk
- gets the client moving
- creates proof
- teaches the client how The AI Plug works
- can expand later
- avoids overwhelming the buyer

Do not use the starter to undercharge for full-system work.

---

## Chapter 24 field script

Use this when simplifying options:

> I would not give you ten options. There are really three paths: a review if we need clarity, a starter system if one problem is obvious, or a full growth system if multiple pieces need to connect.

Bridge question:

> Which path feels right — clarity first, one first system, or a larger connected build?

---

## Chapter 24 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can package the offer into Review, Starter, and Growth.

A rep fails if they overwhelm the client with every possible service.

---

# Chapter 25 — How To Present Price

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches reps how to talk about price without sounding apologetic, vague, or pushy.

The rep should make price feel connected to scope and value.

---

## The price conversation rule

Do not hide price forever.

Do not throw out random numbers too early.

The right order is:

1. Understand the business.
2. Identify the problem.
3. Decide the first useful system.
4. Explain scope.
5. Give price range or proposal.
6. Explain what is included and excluded.
7. Confirm next step.

---

## How to introduce price ranges

Use this:

> Based on what you described, this sounds like a starter system, not a full build. Starter systems usually fall between [range], depending on pages, content, forms, routing, AI answers, and testing.

For full system:

> A full AI Growth System is a larger build. Those usually start around [range] and go up depending on the number of pages, workflows, AI assistants, answer libraries, integrations, and monthly support.

For review:

> If we are not sure where AI fits yet, the review is the safest first step. That usually falls between [range], and it gives us the roadmap before building.

---

## How to avoid price shock

Before giving price, frame the work.

Bad:

> It is $5,000.

Better:

> The starter system is $5,000.

Best:

> Because this includes strategy, page/copy work, the intake flow, routing, approval language, mobile testing, and launch QA, this starter build would be $5,000.

The client should understand what they are buying.

---

## How to avoid sounding cheap

Do not say:

- “It is only…”
- “I can do it cheap.”
- “I know it is expensive.”
- “I can cut the price.”
- “I am just starting.”

Instead say:

- “The safest first step is…”
- “The scope includes…”
- “The reason this price changes is…”
- “We can start smaller if needed.”
- “This is the setup cost. Monthly support is separate.”

---

## How to offer a smaller path

If the client hesitates, do not immediately discount.

Offer a smaller scope.

Use this:

> We can reduce the scope without pretending the full system costs less. The smaller version would focus only on [first useful system] and leave [future pieces] for later.

This protects value.

---

## How to explain monthly

Use this:

> The setup builds the first version. Monthly support keeps it improving — content, answer updates, workflow improvements, campaign support, or AI adaptation depending on the plan.

Do not call monthly “maintenance” if that makes it sound small.

Call it:

- AI Growth Management
- monthly AI adaptation
- ongoing optimization
- content/workflow support
- growth support

---

## Chapter 25 field script

Use this when the client asks, “What does something like this cost?”

> It depends on whether we are reviewing, building one starter system, or connecting multiple systems. A review is the smallest step. A starter system is usually the best first build. A full AI Growth System costs more because it connects marketing, customer answers, intake, automation, internal assistants, and ongoing improvement.

Bridge question:

> Do you want a price for the safest first step or the full version?

---

## Chapter 25 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can present price with confidence and scope clarity.

A rep fails if they discount before changing scope.

---

# Chapter 26 — Pricing Objections

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches how to respond to pricing concerns without sounding defensive or desperate.

Price objections are normal.

The rep’s job is to clarify value, adjust scope if needed, and protect the offer.

---

## Objection: “That sounds expensive.”

Bad response:

> I can lower it.

Better response:

> We can start smaller.

Best response:

> I understand. That is why I would separate the full system from the first useful system. If the full scope is too much right now, we can start with the one area that matters most and leave the rest for later.

---

## Objection: “Can you do it cheaper?”

Bad response:

> Maybe. What is your budget?

Better response:

> We can reduce scope.

Best response:

> I do not want to pretend the same scope costs less. But we can absolutely reduce the scope. For example, instead of website, content, AI answers, intake, and monthly management, we can start with just the review or one starter system.

---

## Objection: “I can get a website cheaper.”

Bad response:

> Cheap websites are bad.

Better response:

> You can definitely get a cheaper website.

Best response:

> That is true. If you only want a basic website, there are cheaper options. What we are building is different: strategy, AI use-case planning, conversion copy, request flow, customer answer planning, launch testing, and a system that can grow.

---

## Objection: “I can use ChatGPT myself.”

Bad response:

> Not like we do.

Better response:

> You can use ChatGPT.

Best response:

> You can, and you should. The difference is turning AI into a business-specific system — with your services, approved answers, prompts, templates, workflows, staff use cases, and guardrails.

---

## Objection: “I do not want monthly.”

Bad response:

> Then it will not work.

Better response:

> We can do one-time.

Best response:

> We can scope a one-time build. Monthly is only needed if you want ongoing content, updates, AI answer improvements, workflow support, campaign help, or AI adaptation as tools change.

---

## Objection: “Can you guarantee leads?”

Bad response:

> We should get you leads.

Better response:

> No, we do not guarantee leads.

Best response:

> No. Leads depend on the market, competition, timing, reputation, and offer. What we can do is build a stronger system for marketing, customer answers, intake, follow-up, and ongoing improvement.

---

## Objection: “I need to think about it.”

Bad response:

> What part do you need to think about?

Better response:

> Of course.

Best response:

> Of course. I will send a short summary with the problem, the recommended first system, the price, what is included, what is excluded, and what the next step would be. That way you are reviewing the actual decision, not trying to remember the whole conversation.

---

## Objection: “Send me information.”

Bad response:

> Here is our website.

Better response:

> I will send info.

Best response:

> Absolutely. I will send a short recap focused on your business: what we heard, where AI may help first, the recommended starting point, price range, and what would be needed to move forward.

---

## Objection: “We are not ready.”

Bad response:

> When will you be ready?

Better response:

> We can follow up later.

Best response:

> That may be the right call. If you are not ready for a build, the smallest next step could be an AI Growth Review, or we can pause and revisit when you are ready to look at AI seriously.

---

## Chapter 26 field script

Use this when the client hesitates on price:

> We do not have to force the full version. The decision is whether you want clarity first, one starter system, or the larger connected build. We can reduce scope, but I do not want to pretend a full system is the same price as a starter.

Bridge question:

> Would it make more sense to start with the review or with one smaller system?

---

## Chapter 26 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they protect value and reduce scope instead of discounting blindly.

A rep fails if they make promises to justify price.

---

# Chapter 27 — Proposal Pricing Structure

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches how to write a clean proposal that explains price, scope, exclusions, and next steps.

A strong proposal prevents confusion.

---

## Proposal structure

Every proposal should include:

1. Business summary
2. Problem diagnosed
3. Recommended first system
4. Why this is the right first move
5. Scope included
6. Scope excluded
7. Required client approvals
8. Timeline estimate
9. Setup price
10. Monthly price if applicable
11. Pass-through costs
12. Guardrails
13. Next step

---

## Proposal section 1 — Business summary

Example:

> Based on our conversation, your business needs a clearer way to use AI in the first area that can create practical value: [marketing / customer answers / intake / internal assistant / automation / full system].

---

## Proposal section 2 — Problem diagnosed

Example:

> The main issue appears to be [problem]. This creates friction because [plain-English explanation].

---

## Proposal section 3 — Recommended first system

Example:

> We recommend starting with [offer] because it addresses the first useful AI opportunity without overbuilding.

---

## Proposal section 4 — Scope included

Include bullets like:

- strategy session
- copy/messaging
- page/flow design
- form/intake setup
- answer library
- assistant prompt setup
- routing
- launch testing
- approval pass
- handoff guide
- monthly support if included

---

## Proposal section 5 — Scope excluded

Be clear.

Examples:

- ad spend not included
- third-party tool subscriptions not included
- unlimited revisions not included
- professional/legal/medical/financial advice not included
- guaranteed outcomes not included
- major custom software not included unless listed
- customer-facing AI answers require approval

---

## Proposal section 6 — Price

Use this format:

**Setup / build fee:** $X  
**Monthly support:** $X/month  
**Pass-through costs:** listed separately  
**Payment schedule:** deposit / milestone / monthly

---

## Proposal section 7 — Guardrails

Use this:

> This proposal does not guarantee leads, traffic, rankings, revenue, bookings, appointments, jobs, reviews, or ad performance. AI systems require approved answers, human control where judgment matters, and client approval before launch.

---

## Proposal section 8 — Next step

Use this:

> To move forward, approve the scope, pay the deposit, and provide the requested business details/assets. After that, we schedule kickoff and confirm the approval path.

---

## Example starter proposal summary

> Based on our conversation, I recommend starting with an AI Growth Review and Smart Intake Starter. The goal is to identify the first practical AI use case, then build a cleaner request flow that captures the right details and routes them to the right person. This is not a full AI Growth System yet. It does not include ad spend, guaranteed leads, or unsupervised customer-facing AI. It gives us a safe first system that can expand later.

---

## Example full proposal summary

> Based on our conversation, your business appears ready for a phased Full AI Growth System. The first phase will review the business, clarify the strongest AI opportunities, and build the foundation. Later phases may connect marketing, customer answers, intake, automation, internal assistants, and monthly management. This will be scoped in phases so we do not overbuild before the first useful system is proven.

---

## Chapter 27 field script

Use this when sending a proposal:

> I am going to send this in a way that is clear: what problem we are solving, what system I recommend first, what is included, what is excluded, the price, and what the next step is. I do not want this to feel like a vague tech proposal.

Bridge question:

> Do you want the proposal written around the smallest useful first system, or should I include the larger future phase as an option too?

---

## Chapter 27 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can create a proposal that protects scope, price, and guardrails.

A rep fails if the proposal promises outcomes or hides exclusions.

---

# Part 4 quality-control checklist

Before this section is approved, confirm:

- It prices The AI Plug as AI adoption and growth systems, not a commodity website shop.
- It explains pricing drivers clearly.
- It gives setup and monthly ranges.
- It separates pass-through costs.
- It avoids guarantees.
- It teaches Review / Starter / Growth packaging.
- It tells reps to reduce scope before discounting.
- It includes proposal structure and exclusions.
- It supports future dashboard pricing recommendations.
- It is flexible enough for vertical-specific pricing later.

---

# The AI Plug Master Manual v3 — Part 5: Sales Conversations + Scripts
**AI Advantage First draft**  
**Date:** 2026-05-27  
**Project:** Where To Go SA / The AI Plug  
**Public visitor brand:** Where To Go SA  
**Business systems brand:** The AI Plug  
**Manual type:** Full-depth master business training manual  
**Section covered:** Part 5 — Sales Conversations + Scripts, Chapters 28–35

---

## Direction lock for this section

This section follows the corrected AI Advantage First direction.

The rep must not sound like:

- a generic website salesperson
- a basic digital marketing agency
- a chatbot installer
- an automation vendor
- a lead-generation hustler
- a software demo rep
- an AI hype promoter

The rep should sound like a practical business operator who understands that owners want:

- competitive advantage
- clarity
- speed
- better marketing output
- better customer response
- less repetitive work
- safer AI adoption
- a first useful AI move
- ongoing adaptation as AI changes

The core sales message is:

# The AI Plug helps businesses get an AI advantage before their competitors do.

The job of every script is to make the owner think:

> “This person understands what AI could actually do for my business, and they are not just selling me another website or chatbot.”

---

# Chapter 28 — Sales Conversation Philosophy

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches how every sales conversation should feel.

The AI Plug sales conversation should not start with tools.

It should start with the owner’s business reality.

Bad starting point:

> “We build AI websites, chatbots, forms, dashboards, and automations.”

Better starting point:

> “Where do you think AI could help your business first?”

Best starting point:

> “Most owners know AI is changing business, but they do not know where it fits yet. The safest first step is finding the first useful place AI can help you move faster, answer better, market smarter, or reduce repetitive work.”

---

## The conversation sequence

A strong sales conversation usually follows this order:

1. Establish relevance.
2. Ask what the owner already thinks about AI.
3. Identify the business pressure.
4. Find the first possible AI use case.
5. Translate the problem into a service lane.
6. Recommend the safe first step.
7. Explain scope and guardrails.
8. Discuss price.
9. Confirm next step.
10. Send a clean recap.

---

## The tone

The tone should be:

- confident
- plainspoken
- practical
- curious
- direct
- owner-friendly
- no hype
- no scare tactics
- no guarantees
- no “AI will replace people” language

The rep should avoid sounding like they are trying to impress the owner with jargon.

The rep should sound like they can diagnose the business and build what matters.

---

## The most important sales skill

The most important skill is translation.

The owner may say:

- “I need more customers.”
- “I need better marketing.”
- “We miss calls.”
- “Customers ask the same questions.”
- “My staff is overloaded.”
- “I do not understand AI.”
- “I already have a website.”
- “I do not want a chatbot.”

The rep must translate that into:

- AI Growth Review
- AI Marketing System
- AI Customer Experience
- AI Intake + Automation
- AI Business Assistant
- AI Growth Management
- Full AI Growth System

---

## What not to lead with

Do not lead with:

- “customer path”
- “lead path”
- “dashboard”
- “workflow stack”
- “AI automation pipeline”
- “vertical lane”
- “funnel architecture”
- “lead classification”
- “conversion layer”
- “operating system”

These may be internal planning terms.

They are not strong first-contact sales language.

---

## The better language bank

Use language like:

- “Where should AI help first?”
- “What part of the business should be faster?”
- “What customer questions keep repeating?”
- “What marketing keeps getting delayed?”
- “What does your team write, explain, or follow up on over and over?”
- “What should stay human?”
- “What should AI help prepare, organize, or answer?”
- “What would make the first step feel safe?”
- “Where would you want better visibility?”
- “What would you want reviewed before anything goes live?”

---

## Chapter 28 field script

Use this to frame the whole sales conversation:

> I do not want to start by selling you a website or chatbot. The better question is where AI could help your business first. For some owners, it is marketing. For others, it is customer questions, intake, follow-up, internal assistant work, or ongoing AI support. We should find the first useful move before building anything big.

Bridge question:

> What part of your business feels like it should already be easier, faster, or better supported?

---

## Chapter 28 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they start with the business problem and AI opportunity.

A rep fails if they start with a tool list.

---

# Chapter 29 — Opening Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter gives the rep opening scripts for different situations.

An opener should be short, relevant, and easy to answer.

The goal is not to close in the opener.

The goal is to create a real conversation.

---

## Universal cold opener

> Hey [Name], I’m with The AI Plug. We help businesses figure out where AI can create a practical advantage — marketing, customer questions, intake, automation, internal assistant work, or follow-up. I’m not reaching out to sell a random chatbot. I wanted to ask: are you looking at how AI could fit your business yet?

---

## Shorter cold opener

> Hey [Name], quick question — are you looking at where AI could help your business this year, or is that still on the back burner?

---

## Owner-to-owner style opener

> I work with business owners on practical AI adoption — not hype, not replacing staff, not random tools. The first question is usually where AI can help the business move faster or respond better. Is that something you’ve started looking at?

---

## Local business opener

> I’m local to the San Antonio business market and I’m building AI systems around real business use cases — marketing, customer questions, intake, follow-up, and internal assistant work. I wanted to ask if AI is something you’re trying to figure out for your business.

---

## Website observation opener

Use this when you have reviewed their website.

> I looked at your website and had one practical thought: there may be an opportunity to use AI around [specific area: customer questions / quote requests / content / consultation intake / event inquiries / follow-up]. I’m not saying you need a full rebuild. Would it be worth a quick review of where AI might help first?

---

## Social media observation opener

Use this when you noticed weak or inconsistent content.

> I noticed your business has the kind of knowledge customers probably ask about all the time, but the content does not seem to be coming out consistently. One place AI can help is turning real customer questions into posts, pages, short-form ideas, and campaign content. Is content consistency something you’re trying to improve?

---

## Repeat-question opener

Use this when the business likely answers the same questions.

> Quick question — do customers ask your team the same questions over and over before they book, buy, visit, or request a quote? That is one of the safer first places AI can help, as long as the answers are approved and controlled.

---

## Internal assistant opener

Use this when the business owner or staff may be overloaded.

> One of the best first AI uses is not public at all. It is internal — drafts, summaries, research, follow-up notes, proposals, content prep, and staff training. Is your team repeating any work that AI could help prepare faster?

---

## Paid ads opener

Use this when they may already be spending or interested in paid campaigns.

> Are you running Google Ads or paid social right now? The reason I ask is AI can help with campaign angles, landing pages, content, intake, and follow-up — but ads work better when the system behind them is ready.

---

## Voice/phone opener

Use this when phone calls are important.

> Are missed calls or repeat phone questions a problem in your business? AI phone planning is becoming more important, but it needs clear rules, approved language, and handoff before anything live makes sense.

---

## Conversation opener after an Uber/rider/business conversation

Use this for natural conversations from the user’s real-world lead pipeline.

> I build practical AI systems for businesses — not just websites, but systems for marketing, customer questions, intake, follow-up, internal assistants, and ongoing AI adoption. A lot of owners know AI matters but do not know where to start. That is usually where I help first.

Follow-up question:

> What kind of business are you in, and what part of it would you want AI to make easier?

---

## Chapter 29 field script

Universal safe opener:

> I do not know if this is a fit yet, but I help businesses figure out where AI can create a practical advantage. The first step is usually not a giant build — it is identifying the first useful place AI can help.

Bridge question:

> Are you already thinking about AI for the business, or have you mostly been ignoring it for now?

---

## Chapter 29 training check

A rep passes this chapter if their opener creates curiosity without hype.

A rep fails if the opener sounds like spam, a chatbot pitch, or a guaranteed-lead pitch.

---

# Chapter 30 — Discovery Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches discovery.

Discovery is where the rep earns the right to recommend.

The rep should not recommend a service lane before understanding the owner’s business reality.

---

## Universal discovery opener

> Before I recommend anything, I want to understand how the business works. Where do customers usually find you, what do they ask before taking action, and what part of the process feels slower than it should?

---

## The AI awareness questions

Ask:

- What have you already tried with AI?
- Are you using ChatGPT or any AI tools now?
- What part of AI feels useful to you?
- What part feels like hype?
- What are you worried AI might get wrong?
- Do you want AI mostly for marketing, customer response, internal work, or automation?
- Would your staff use AI if it made one task easier?

---

## The business pressure questions

Ask:

- What is the biggest thing you want the business to improve this year?
- Do you need more attention, better response, better follow-up, or less internal busywork?
- What keeps getting delayed because the owner or staff is busy?
- What customer questions come up repeatedly?
- What does your team write or explain over and over?
- Where do requests or opportunities get messy?
- What would make a customer more likely to take the next step?

---

## Marketing discovery

Ask:

- How do most customers find you now?
- Is your website doing enough to explain the business?
- Are you creating content consistently?
- Do you need blogs, service pages, short-form video ideas, or social posts?
- Are you trying to show up in local search?
- Are customers finding you through Google, social, referrals, or word of mouth?
- Are you running paid ads?
- Do ads connect to a strong landing page and request path?
- What would you publish every week if it was easier?

---

## Customer experience discovery

Ask:

- What questions do customers ask before they book, visit, call, or request a quote?
- Which answers are safe to give automatically?
- Which answers need a person?
- What should AI never say?
- Do customers know what next step to take?
- Would an approved answer library help?
- Would a website operator help guide people?
- Do you want chat, voice planning, or just better FAQ/support language first?

---

## Intake + automation discovery

Ask:

- How do requests come in today?
- What details are usually missing?
- Who sees the request first?
- Who should see it?
- What happens if nobody follows up?
- Are there photos, documents, dates, guest counts, budgets, or service areas involved?
- Do requests come through calls, forms, DMs, texts, email, or in person?
- What should happen after a request comes in?
- What should be automated, and what should stay human?

---

## Internal assistant discovery

Ask:

- What does the owner or staff write repeatedly?
- What do you summarize repeatedly?
- What do you research repeatedly?
- Do you need proposal help?
- Do you need follow-up drafts?
- Do you need content drafts?
- Do you need training scripts or SOPs?
- Do you need a daily or weekly brief?
- What would save the most owner time if AI helped prepare it?

---

## Growth management discovery

Ask:

- Do you want a one-time build, or ongoing help?
- Who will update content, AI answers, and workflows after launch?
- Do you want monthly optimization?
- Do you want someone watching new AI use cases for your business?
- Would monthly recap and next-step recommendations help?
- Do you want AI adoption to be an ongoing part of the business?

---

## Guardrail discovery

Ask:

- Are there legal, medical, financial, insurance, safety, or compliance boundaries?
- What should AI never promise?
- What should require approval?
- Who approves public-facing answers?
- Who approves content?
- Who approves offers and pricing language?
- What should never be automated?
- Where must staff stay in control?

---

## Discovery close

Use this at the end of discovery:

> Based on what you told me, I would not start with everything. The first useful AI move looks like [recommendation]. That addresses [problem] without overbuilding.

Then ask:

> Does that match what you are feeling, or is another area more urgent?

---

## Chapter 30 field script

Use this when starting a diagnostic conversation:

> I’m going to ask a few questions before recommending anything. The goal is to figure out whether AI should help with marketing, customer answers, intake, internal assistant work, automation, or ongoing improvement — and what should wait.

Bridge question:

> Where do you feel the most friction right now?

---

## Chapter 30 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they ask enough questions to recommend a first useful AI move.

A rep fails if they recommend before diagnosis.

---

# Chapter 31 — Offer Explanation Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter gives scripts for explaining each offer simply.

The owner should understand the offer without needing technical knowledge.

---

## AI Growth Review script

> The AI Growth Review is the safest first step when you know AI matters but do not know where to start. We look at marketing, customer questions, intake, follow-up, internal work, and automation opportunities. Then we recommend the first practical AI system instead of guessing.

Short version:

> It keeps us from selling you the wrong thing.

---

## AI Marketing Systems script

> AI Marketing Systems help your business create stronger marketing more consistently. That can include pages, blogs, SEO, AI search visibility, social content, short-form ideas, campaign assets, landing pages, and paid campaign support when appropriate. The goal is not generic AI content. The goal is to turn your real business knowledge into useful marketing faster.

Short version:

> Better marketing output without starting from zero every week.

---

## AI Customer Experience script

> AI Customer Experience is not just a chatbot. It is approved customer guidance — FAQs, answer libraries, guided questions, website operators, or voice planning — with clear rules about what AI can say and when a person needs to step in.

Short version:

> Faster answers, but with boundaries.

---

## AI Intake + Automation script

> AI Intake + Automation helps collect better information before your team has to chase it. That can mean smart forms, photo intake, event intake, quote request flows, alerts, routing, follow-up drafts, QR paths, or simple automations. The point is not forms. The point is cleaner information and next steps.

Short version:

> Better information in, cleaner next step out.

---

## AI Business Assistants script

> AI Business Assistants support the internal work your team repeats: drafts, summaries, proposals, research, follow-up notes, content prep, SOPs, training, and daily briefs. This is often the safest AI starting point because it helps staff before AI ever talks directly to customers.

Short version:

> Less starting from scratch.

---

## AI Growth Management script

> AI Growth Management is ongoing support after the first build. AI changes fast, and the system should not go stale. Monthly support can include content updates, answer updates, workflow improvements, campaign support, internal assistant improvements, and new AI opportunity reviews.

Short version:

> Keep improving instead of rebuilding from scratch.

---

## Full AI Growth System script

> A Full AI Growth System connects the bigger pieces: marketing, customer answers, intake, automation, internal assistants, owner visibility, and ongoing AI improvement. It is not the first move for everyone. It makes sense when the business has multiple connected problems and is ready for a phased system.

Short version:

> The connected version when the business is ready.

---

## Paid Campaign + Landing Page script

> Paid campaigns can help, but only when the system behind them is ready. We look at the offer, landing page, intake, customer answers, follow-up, and tracking before treating ads like the whole solution.

Short version:

> Ads work better when the page and follow-up are ready.

---

## AI Phone / Voice Planning script

> AI phone and voice tools are getting more important, but they need planning before going live. We define what calls AI can handle, what it must never say, what information to collect, and when to hand off to a person.

Short version:

> Voice planning before voice automation.

---

## Chapter 31 field script

Use this when explaining the lane options:

> There are a few possible lanes, but I would not throw all of them at you. Based on what you said, the best fit is probably [lane]. That would help with [owner problem] and can expand later if it works.

Bridge question:

> Do you want to start with the review, or does this first system already feel clear?

---

## Chapter 31 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can explain each service lane in plain language.

A rep fails if they describe everything as websites, forms, or dashboards.

---

# Chapter 32 — Follow-Up Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter gives practical follow-up language.

Follow-up should feel useful, not desperate.

Every follow-up should connect back to:

- the owner’s stated problem
- the recommended first useful move
- the safe next step
- no guarantees
- scope clarity

---

## Follow-up after first conversation

Subject:

> Quick recap on where AI might help first

Message:

> Hey [Name], good talking with you. Based on what you shared, I would not start with a giant AI build. The first useful move looks like [recommendation] because [reason]. That would help with [business problem] while keeping [guardrail / human approval / scope] clear.

> The next step would be [AI Growth Review / starter system / short follow-up call].

---

## Follow-up after AI Growth Review discussion

> Hey [Name], the review would help us avoid guessing. We would look at marketing, customer questions, intake, follow-up, internal work, and automation opportunities, then recommend the first practical AI system.

> That is the safer move if you know AI matters but are not sure where it fits yet.

---

## Follow-up after discovery call

> Hey [Name], based on the discovery call, the clearest opportunity seems to be [problem]. I would recommend starting with [offer] instead of trying to build everything at once.

> That first step would include [scope] and would leave [future items] for later.

---

## Follow-up after proposal

> Hey [Name], just checking back on the proposal. I kept the recommendation focused on [first useful system], not a full rebuild. The goal is to solve [problem] first, then decide whether it is worth expanding.

> Do you want to review it together, adjust the scope, or pause for now?

---

## Follow-up after no response

> Hey [Name], I do not want to keep bugging you. Should I keep this open, or would it be better to circle back later?

---

## Follow-up after “send me information”

> Absolutely. I’ll send a short recap focused on your business, not a generic brochure: what we heard, where AI may help first, the recommended starting point, the scope, and what would be needed to move forward.

---

## Follow-up after price hesitation

> I understand. We do not need to force the full version. We can either start with the review or reduce the scope to one starter system. I would rather make the first step smaller than pretend the full system costs less.

---

## Follow-up after “not now”

> That makes sense. I’ll mark this as not now. If AI becomes a priority later, the best place to start would probably be [review / first system], because that gives you clarity before spending on a larger build.

---

## Follow-up after launch

> The first version is live/tested. The next thing to watch is whether [specific workflow] feels clearer and whether the team has enough visibility into [requests / answers / content / follow-up]. I’ll make notes for the first recap.

---

## Monthly recap follow-up

> Here is the monthly recap: what was updated, what we noticed, what needs attention, and the next practical AI opportunity. The goal is to keep the system improving instead of letting it go stale.

---

## Chapter 32 field script

Use this before sending a follow-up:

> I am going to keep this focused on what we actually discussed: the problem, the first useful system, what is included, what is not included, and the next step.

Bridge question:

> Do you want me to include a smaller starter option or only the main recommendation?

---

## Chapter 32 training check

A rep passes this chapter if follow-up feels like a useful recap.

A rep fails if follow-up feels like begging, hype, or pressure.

---

# Chapter 33 — Close + Next-Step Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches how to ask for the next step.

Closing should feel natural.

The rep should not force urgency.

The rep should make the next action clear.

---

## Close for AI Growth Review

> The safest next step is the AI Growth Review. That lets us look at the business, identify the first useful AI move, and avoid guessing. If you want to move forward, I’ll send the review scope, price, and what we need from you.

---

## Close for Starter System

> The first useful system is [offer]. It solves [problem] without building everything at once. If you want to move forward, the next step is scope approval, deposit, and the kickoff details.

---

## Close for Full AI Growth System

> This looks bigger than one small fix, but I would still phase it. The next step is to approve the first phase so we can confirm the roadmap and build the first useful system before expanding.

---

## Close for monthly management

> The setup gets the first version live. Monthly management keeps improving it — content, answers, workflows, assistant updates, reporting, and new AI opportunities. If you want ongoing support, I’ll include that as a separate monthly line item.

---

## Low-pressure close

> No pressure to build everything right now. The decision is whether this first step is worth doing. If it is, we can start there and leave the bigger pieces for later.

---

## Scope-reduction close

> We can reduce the scope, but I would not pretend the full system costs less. The smaller version would focus only on [starter system] and leave [future pieces] out for now.

---

## Decision close

> Does it make sense to start with the review, build the first useful system, or pause and revisit later?

---

## Proposal approval close

> If the scope looks right, the next step is approval and deposit. After that, we schedule kickoff, collect the business details, and confirm who approves the customer-facing language.

---

## Not-a-fit close

> Based on what you want, I do not think we are the right fit if the expectation is guaranteed leads, rankings, revenue, or fully automated customer messages without approval. If you want a practical system with clear guardrails, we can help.

---

## Chapter 33 field script

Use this when the conversation needs direction:

> There are really three choices: review first, build one starter system, or pause. I would recommend [choice] based on what you told me.

Bridge question:

> Do you want to move forward with that, or should we adjust the scope?

---

## Chapter 33 training check

A rep passes this chapter if the next step is clear and safe.

A rep fails if they pressure, promise results, or avoid asking for the decision.

---

# Chapter 34 — Objection Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter gives objection responses aligned with the AI Advantage First direction.

Every response should:

1. Acknowledge the concern.
2. Avoid arguing.
3. Reframe around the business problem.
4. Explain the safe first step.
5. Avoid guarantees.
6. End with a simple next action.

---

## Objection: “I already have a website.”

Bad response:

> We can build a better one.

Better response:

> That is good. The website may not be the issue.

Best response:

> That makes sense. Most businesses already have a website. The question is whether the website is helping with the next step: customer questions, intake, content, follow-up, and conversion. Sometimes the first AI move is not a new website. It may be content, customer answers, intake, or an internal assistant.

Next action:

> We can review where the current site is helping and where AI could support the next step.

---

## Objection: “I do not need AI.”

Bad response:

> Every business needs AI.

Better response:

> You may not need AI everywhere.

Best response:

> That is fair. You may not need AI everywhere. The question is whether there is one place AI could help the business move faster, answer better, publish more consistently, or reduce repetitive work. If there is not, we should not force it.

Next action:

> A short review would tell us if there is a useful first move or not.

---

## Objection: “I do not want a chatbot.”

Bad response:

> It is not a chatbot; it is an AI operator.

Better response:

> We do not have to start with chat.

Best response:

> I get that. A lot of chatbots are annoying. We do not have to start with chat. AI might be more useful for content, internal drafts, customer answer prep, intake, or follow-up. If customer-facing AI ever makes sense, it should use approved answers and human handoff.

Next action:

> Let’s look at the best first AI use case before deciding on chat.

---

## Objection: “Can you guarantee leads?”

Bad response:

> We should be able to get leads.

Better response:

> No, we cannot guarantee leads.

Best response:

> No. Leads depend on the market, competition, timing, reputation, and the offer. What we can do is build a stronger system for marketing, customer answers, intake, follow-up, and ongoing improvement. That gives the business a better foundation, but it does not guarantee outcomes.

Next action:

> We can scope the system around what you can control.

---

## Objection: “This sounds expensive.”

Bad response:

> I can lower the price.

Better response:

> We can start smaller.

Best response:

> I understand. That is why I would separate the full system from the first useful system. If the larger scope is too much right now, we can start with a review or one starter system and leave the rest for later.

Next action:

> Do you want me to price the smaller first step?

---

## Objection: “I can use ChatGPT myself.”

Bad response:

> Not like we do.

Better response:

> You can, and you should.

Best response:

> You can, and you should. The difference is turning AI into a business-specific system: your services, approved answers, content structure, prompts, workflows, staff use cases, and guardrails. We help make it usable inside the business.

Next action:

> We can start by identifying where ChatGPT or an AI assistant would help your team most.

---

## Objection: “My staff already handles this.”

Bad response:

> AI can do it better.

Better response:

> Good. AI can support them.

Best response:

> That is good. The goal is not to replace staff. The question is whether AI can help them move faster on repeat tasks — drafts, summaries, customer questions, content prep, follow-up notes, or intake organization.

Next action:

> Let’s identify one repeated task that would help the team if it were easier.

---

## Objection: “We already have software.”

Bad response:

> Our system is better.

Better response:

> We may not need to replace it.

Best response:

> That can be a good thing. We do not need to replace software that works. The question is whether AI can support the gaps around it: content, customer questions, intake, follow-up, internal summaries, or workflows your current software does not make easier.

Next action:

> We can review what your current tools already handle and what still feels manual.

---

## Objection: “We tried marketing before.”

Bad response:

> This is different because AI.

Better response:

> A lot of marketing feels disconnected.

Best response:

> I understand. A lot of marketing feels like activity without a system. AI can help when it is tied to your real customer questions, offers, landing pages, content rhythm, intake, and follow-up. If those pieces are not connected, marketing feels frustrating.

Next action:

> We can start by reviewing whether marketing or the system behind it is the real issue.

---

## Objection: “I do not have time.”

Bad response:

> That is why you need this.

Better response:

> We can keep it simple.

Best response:

> That is exactly why the first step should be simple. We should not add another thing to manage. The first system should reduce friction — content prep, customer answers, intake, summaries, or follow-up — not create more work.

Next action:

> Let’s pick one area that would save time first.

---

## Objection: “Send me information.”

Bad response:

> Here is our website.

Better response:

> I’ll send a summary.

Best response:

> Absolutely. I’ll send a short recap focused on your business: what we heard, where AI may help first, the recommended first step, what is included, what is excluded, and the next action.

Next action:

> I’ll send that over and follow up after you have had time to review it.

---

## Objection: “I need to think about it.”

Bad response:

> What do you need to think about?

Better response:

> Of course.

Best response:

> Of course. I’ll send a recap so you are thinking about the actual decision: the problem, the first useful system, the price, what is included, what is excluded, and what happens next.

Next action:

> Should I check back in a few days, or would next week be better?

---

## Objection: “We are too small for this.”

Bad response:

> Every business needs AI.

Better response:

> You may not need a full system.

Best response:

> You may not need a full system. Smaller businesses usually benefit from one practical AI use case first — content support, customer answer prep, intake, follow-up drafts, or an internal assistant. We should not overbuild.

Next action:

> Let’s look at the smallest useful first move.

---

## Objection: “How do I know this will work?”

Bad response:

> Trust us.

Better response:

> We test the first system.

Best response:

> The honest answer is we start with something specific and testable. We do not guarantee market outcomes. We identify the first useful AI opportunity, build that piece, test the workflow, and decide what should improve next.

Next action:

> The AI Growth Review can identify the best testable first move.

---

## Objection: “Will AI replace people?”

Bad response:

> It can replace repetitive work.

Better response:

> That is not how we position it.

Best response:

> No. We position AI as support for people — drafts, summaries, content prep, approved answers, intake organization, and workflow help. Judgment, relationships, approval, and final decisions stay with the business.

Next action:

> Let’s focus on support tasks, not replacement.

---

## Objection: “What if AI says something wrong?”

Bad response:

> We can control that.

Better response:

> That is why guardrails matter.

Best response:

> That is the right concern. Customer-facing AI should use approved answers, clear boundaries, and human handoff. In some cases, the better first move is internal AI support before AI ever talks to customers.

Next action:

> Let’s identify what answers are safe, what requires a person, and what should never be automated.

---

## Chapter 34 field script

Use this objection framework:

> I hear you. The way I would think about it is [reframe]. We do not need to force the full version. The safer next step is [review / first useful system].

Bridge question:

> Would starting smaller make more sense?

---

## Chapter 34 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they handle objections without arguing or promising outcomes.

A rep fails if they discount too early, promise leads, or defend AI instead of solving the owner’s concern.

---

# Chapter 35 — Sales Recap + Handoff Scripts

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches how to summarize a sales conversation so nothing gets lost.

A good recap helps:

- the client understand the decision
- the rep protect scope
- the delivery team understand what to build
- the dashboard classify the lead correctly
- future follow-up stay focused

---

## Client-facing recap structure

Use this structure:

1. What we heard.
2. What problem appears most important.
3. Where AI may help first.
4. What we recommend.
5. What is included.
6. What is not included.
7. What we need from you.
8. Next step.

---

## Client-facing recap template

> Hey [Name], here is the recap from our conversation.

> What I heard: [business reality].

> The biggest opportunity appears to be [problem].

> The first useful AI move would be [recommendation] because [reason].

> This would include [scope].

> This would not include [exclusions / guarantees / future pieces].

> To move forward, we would need [assets / approval / deposit / kickoff].

> The next step is [specific next step].

---

## Internal handoff structure

Use this structure after every serious conversation:

- Business name:
- Contact:
- Business type:
- Website/social:
- Current tools:
- Main problem:
- Owner’s words:
- AI awareness level:
- Recommended first system:
- Service lane:
- Why this fits:
- What should wait:
- Guardrail risks:
- Approval needs:
- Pricing discussed:
- Next action:
- Follow-up date:
- Notes for delivery:

---

## Dashboard tags

Use these tags where helpful:

### Business type

- Home Services + Contractors
- Hospitality + Local Experience
- Professional Services
- Transportation + Concierge
- Restaurants
- Local Brands + Retail
- Other / Custom

### Service lane

- AI Growth Review
- AI Marketing Systems
- AI Customer Experience
- AI Intake + Automation
- AI Business Assistants
- AI Growth Management
- Full AI Growth System

### Starting point

- AI Website + Conversion System
- AI Content + Social Media Engine
- SEO + AI Search Visibility
- Paid Campaign + Landing Page System
- AI Chat Operator
- AI Phone / Voice Planning
- Smart Intake + Automation
- Internal AI Assistants + Workflow Support

### Guardrail risk

- low
- medium
- high

High risk examples:

- legal
- medical
- tax
- financial
- insurance
- safety-sensitive
- transportation availability
- emergency claims
- customer-facing AI with unapproved answers

---

## Proposal handoff template

> Recommended proposal: [offer].

> Problem diagnosed: [problem].

> Why this first: [reason].

> Scope: [included].

> Exclusions: [not included].

> Guardrails: [no guarantees / approval rules / human handoff].

> Pricing direction: [range or quoted amount].

> Next step: [approval / review / kickoff / follow-up].

---

## Delivery handoff template

> Client approved [offer]. Build should focus on [problem]. Do not build [items excluded]. Client must approve [copy / answers / forms / automation / customer-facing language] before launch. Guardrails include [industry boundaries]. First QA should test [specific workflow].

---

## Follow-up handoff template

> Current status: [stage]. Client is considering [offer]. Main concern is [objection]. Best next message should focus on [reframe]. Do not pressure. Offer [review / smaller scope / call] as next step.

---

## Chapter 35 field script

Use this before ending a call:

> I’m going to send a short recap so we are both clear: what problem we identified, what I recommend first, what is included, what is not included, and what the next step would be.

Bridge question:

> Is there anything you want me to include or clarify before I send it?

---

## Chapter 35 training check

A rep passes this chapter if the recap is clear enough for another person to continue the sale or build.

A rep fails if the recap is vague or only says “client interested in AI.”

---

# Part 5 quality-control checklist

Before this section is approved, confirm:

- Scripts sound like a real business conversation.
- The rep does not lead with websites, dashboards, forms, or chatbots.
- AI advantage and practical adoption are central.
- Discovery questions identify the first useful AI move.
- Offer scripts explain each lane in plain language.
- Objection handling avoids guarantees.
- Follow-up does not sound desperate.
- Close scripts create clear next steps.
- Recap and handoff templates support dashboard classification.
- Language does not collapse into old lead-path-only positioning.

---

# The AI Plug Master Manual v3 — Part 6: Vertical Translation Framework
**AI Advantage First draft**  
**Date:** 2026-05-27  
**Project:** Where To Go SA / The AI Plug  
**Public visitor brand:** Where To Go SA  
**Business systems brand:** The AI Plug  
**Manual type:** Full-depth master business training manual  
**Section covered:** Part 6 — Vertical Translation Framework, Chapters 36–44

---

## Direction lock for this section

This section follows the corrected AI Advantage First direction.

The AI Plug must not sell the same pitch to every business.

A roofer, restaurant, attorney, transportation provider, resort-area business, local retailer, bar, event venue, consultant, and remodeler do not buy AI for the same reason.

The master offer is the same:

# The AI Plug helps businesses get an AI advantage before their competitors do.

But the translation must change by business type.

The rep’s job is to translate AI into the owner’s world:

- what they sell
- how customers decide
- where work slows down
- where staff repeats work
- where customer questions pile up
- where content and marketing fall behind
- where AI could help first
- what should stay human
- what should not be promised

---

# Chapter 36 — Why Vertical Translation Matters

## Chapter purpose

This chapter teaches why one generic AI pitch will not sell well.

The AI Plug can have one master strategy, but every vertical needs its own language.

If the rep says:

> “We build AI systems for marketing, intake, customer experience, automation, and internal assistants.”

That may be true, but it may not catch the owner.

The owner wants to hear:

- “Can this help my roofing company handle inspection requests faster?”
- “Can this help my restaurant answer private event questions?”
- “Can this help my law firm qualify consultations safely?”
- “Can this help my HVAC company follow up on replacement quotes?”
- “Can this help my event venue handle group inquiries?”
- “Can this help my transportation business collect pickup details?”

The rep must speak the owner’s world.

---

## The translation rule

For every business type, translate The AI Plug into five questions:

1. **What does this business actually sell?**
2. **What does the customer need before they say yes?**
3. **Where does the business lose time, trust, or opportunity?**
4. **Where can AI create the first practical advantage?**
5. **What should not be automated or promised?**

If the rep can answer those five questions, the pitch becomes specific.

---

## The wrong approach

Bad:

> We build AI websites, chatbots, automations, and dashboards.

Better:

> We help businesses use AI to improve marketing, customer questions, intake, follow-up, and internal work.

Best:

> For your type of business, the first AI opportunity is probably [specific business problem], because [owner pain], and the safest first system would be [service lane].

---

## Why generic AI language fails

Generic AI language fails because owners hear it as:

- hype
- another tool
- another platform
- another agency pitch
- another cost
- another thing to manage

Specific vertical language feels different.

It says:

> “I understand how your business actually works.”

That is what opens the door.

---

## Chapter 36 field script

Use this when entering a new vertical:

> I do not want to give every business the same AI pitch. The first question is how your type of business actually gets customers, answers questions, handles requests, follows up, and manages internal work. Then we decide where AI fits first.

Bridge question:

> What part of your business has the most repeat work or customer confusion right now?

---

## Chapter 36 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can customize the pitch by business type.

A rep fails if every pitch sounds the same.

---

# Chapter 37 — Universal Vertical Translation Template

## Chapter purpose

This chapter gives the reusable template for every vertical manual.

Each vertical manual should be built from this structure so every business type gets the same depth as the restaurant program.

---

## Vertical manual structure

Every vertical manual should include:

1. Business reality
2. What the owner actually sells
3. What the customer needs before saying yes
4. Where opportunities or productivity leak
5. The AI advantage areas for that vertical
6. Best first offer logic
7. Discovery questions
8. Pitches
9. Outreach scripts
10. Objection handling
11. Pricing logic
12. Proposal summaries
13. Intake checklist
14. Delivery checklist
15. Launch QA checklist
16. Monthly recap logic
17. Renewal / upsell path
18. Dashboard-ready tags
19. Safe claims / do-not-say rules
20. Certification test

---

## Section 1 — Business reality

Explain the business in plain owner language.

Questions:

- Who is the owner?
- What does the business sell?
- How does the business get customers?
- What makes customers trust them?
- What causes friction?
- What does the owner worry about?

Example:

> A home service contractor does not sell “AI.” They sell trust, response speed, clear estimates, service-area confidence, job details, scheduling, and follow-through.

---

## Section 2 — Customer decision reality

Explain what the customer needs before taking action.

Questions:

- What does the customer need to understand?
- What questions do they ask?
- What proof do they need?
- What makes them hesitate?
- What information must be collected?
- What next step should be clear?

Example:

> A restaurant guest planning a private event needs date, guest count, budget, menu options, availability, room fit, and follow-up from the right person.

---

## Section 3 — AI advantage areas

Translate the six master AI advantage areas into the vertical.

The six master areas:

1. AI-Powered Marketing
2. AI Customer Experience
3. AI Intake + Automation
4. AI Business Assistants
5. AI Operations + Owner Visibility
6. Ongoing AI Adaptation

For every vertical, explain what each one means.

---

## Section 4 — Best first offer logic

Every vertical needs a recommendation map.

Example format:

- If the owner is unclear → AI Growth Review
- If content is weak → AI Marketing Systems
- If customers ask repeated questions → AI Customer Experience
- If request details are messy → AI Intake + Automation
- If staff repeats internal work → AI Business Assistant
- If multiple pieces are connected → Full AI Growth System
- If the business wants continued support → AI Growth Management

Then customize by vertical.

---

## Section 5 — Safe claims

Every vertical must include safe claims.

Universal do-not-promise list:

- guaranteed leads
- guaranteed traffic
- guaranteed rankings
- guaranteed revenue
- guaranteed reservations
- guaranteed bookings
- guaranteed appointments
- guaranteed jobs
- guaranteed reviews
- guaranteed social reach
- guaranteed ad ROI
- staff replacement
- unapproved customer-facing AI
- professional advice
- unsafe claims
- regulated outcome promises

Then add vertical-specific guardrails.

---

## Chapter 37 field script

Use this when building a new vertical manual:

> For this vertical, we need to answer: what does the owner sell, what does the customer need, where does work slow down, where can AI help first, and what must stay human?

Bridge question:

> What would this owner immediately recognize as a real problem?

---

## Chapter 37 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can use the vertical template to build a specific sales logic.

A rep fails if the vertical manual only repeats the master manual.

---

# Chapter 38 — Home Services + Contractors Translation

## Chapter purpose

This chapter gives the corrected high-level translation for home services and contractors.

The old home services material is useful, but it leans too hard into customer path and lead leakage as the main identity.

The updated direction keeps those practical details but positions them inside the bigger AI advantage.

---

## Business reality

Home service owners and contractors care about:

- calls
- quote requests
- job photos
- service areas
- scheduling
- estimates
- inspections
- dispatch
- proposal follow-up
- staff handoff
- reviews
- referrals
- seasonal demand
- field crew communication
- owner visibility

They do not want vague AI.

They want something that helps the business move faster and handle requests cleaner.

---

## What they actually sell

Home service businesses sell:

- trust
- response
- qualification
- skill
- timing
- estimates
- project clarity
- service-area confidence
- follow-through
- workmanship
- safety
- reputation

AI must support those things.

---

## What customers need

Homeowners usually need:

- fast response
- clear next step
- service-area confirmation
- basic expectation setting
- quote or inspection path
- photo/document upload when useful
- scheduling clarity
- trust proof
- follow-up

---

## Where AI can create advantage

### AI-Powered Marketing

Home services can use AI for:

- service-area content
- seasonal campaigns
- blog topics from homeowner questions
- local SEO pages
- AI search visibility
- before/after content planning
- social post ideas
- paid campaign landing pages
- offer testing

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help turn your real jobs, services, questions, and service areas into better marketing faster.

---

### AI Customer Experience

Home services can use AI for:

- approved homeowner FAQs
- service selection guidance
- quote request guidance
- what information to prepare
- warranty/process questions
- safe handoff to the team
- chat/operator planning
- voice planning

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help answer common homeowner questions with approved language while your team stays in control.

---

### AI Intake + Automation

Home services can use AI and automation for:

- smart quote forms
- job photo intake
- urgent vs routine sorting
- estimate request routing
- missed-call planning
- reminders
- follow-up draft support
- internal summaries

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help organize the request before your team has to chase missing details.

---

### AI Business Assistants

Home services can use internal assistants for:

- estimate follow-up drafts
- proposal summaries
- homeowner message drafts
- service page drafts
- job summaries
- SOPs
- review request language
- technician handoff notes
- training scripts

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help your team write, summarize, and follow up faster without replacing people.

---

### AI Operations + Owner Visibility

Home services can use owner visibility for:

- new requests
- missed follow-ups
- estimate status
- quote pipeline
- job type trends
- service-area patterns
- review/referral opportunities
- monthly recap

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help you see what needs attention instead of digging through calls, texts, forms, and notes.

---

### Ongoing AI Adaptation

Home services can use ongoing support for:

- seasonal campaigns
- service-area updates
- new FAQ updates
- automation improvements
- quote intake refinements
- AI answer updates
- monthly workflow improvements

Owner-facing language:

> As AI tools improve, we keep finding practical ways to improve your marketing, intake, follow-up, and internal work.

---

## Best first offer logic

- Unclear owner → Home Services AI Growth Review
- Weak marketing → AI Marketing System / SEO + AI Search Visibility
- Repeated homeowner questions → AI Customer Experience Starter
- Messy quote requests → Smart Intake + Automation
- Staff/owner busywork → Internal AI Assistant Starter
- Multiple connected problems → Full Home Services AI Growth System
- Wants ongoing help → AI Growth Management

---

## Key discovery questions

- Where do most requests come from now?
- What questions do homeowners ask repeatedly?
- What information do you wish customers gave you upfront?
- What does your team write or explain over and over?
- Which jobs are worth chasing?
- Which jobs waste time?
- Where does follow-up go cold?
- What part of your business should AI make faster first?
- What should AI never say to a customer?

---

## Safe claims

Do not promise:

- guaranteed jobs
- guaranteed leads
- guaranteed rankings
- guaranteed reviews
- guaranteed emergency response
- financing approvals
- claim approvals
- energy savings
- code compliance outcomes
- AI diagnosis
- staff replacement

---

## Chapter 38 field script

> For home services, AI usually helps in a few places: better marketing from real job questions, faster approved homeowner answers, cleaner quote or photo intake, internal follow-up drafts, and owner visibility into what needs attention. I would not start with everything. I would find the first area where AI can save time or improve response.

Bridge question:

> Are you more worried about marketing, homeowner questions, quote requests, follow-up, or internal busywork?

---

## Chapter 38 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can explain AI advantage in contractor language.

A rep fails if they only talk about forms and lead routing.

## Safe claims

- legal advice

- insurance advice

- guaranteed rankings
- staff replacement

- trip summary drafts
- provider handoff notes
- follow-up drafts
- route content
- customer message drafts
- event/convention notes
- daily route prep

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help prepare trip summaries, route notes, customer follow-up, and provider handoff faster.

---

### AI Operations + Owner Visibility

Transportation businesses can use visibility for:

- new requests
- provider status
- route type
- timing
- follow-up due
- recurring client opportunities
- event/convention demand
- monthly recap

Owner-facing language:

> AI can help the operator see what requests need attention and what details are missing.

---

### Ongoing AI Adaptation

Transportation businesses can use ongoing support for:

- route page updates
- event/convention updates
- request-flow improvements
- approved answer updates
- provider handoff improvements
- AI phone/voice planning
- monthly review

Owner-facing language:

> We keep improving request handling and route content as demand, events, and AI tools change.

---

## Best first offer logic

- Unclear operator → Transportation AI Growth Review
- Needs route visibility → AI Marketing / SEO + AI Search Visibility
- Repeated route questions → AI Customer Experience
- Messy pickup requests → AI Intake + Automation
- Staff/provider handoff work → AI Business Assistant
- Multiple connected needs → Full Transportation AI Growth System
- Wants ongoing support → AI Growth Management

---

## Key discovery questions

- What transportation or concierge requests do you handle?
- How do customers request service today?
- What details are usually missing?
- Who confirms availability?
- What should AI never promise?
- Are you licensed/insured for the services offered?
- Do you serve airport, resorts, downtown, events, or groups?
- How are providers or drivers assigned?
- Do you need route pages or local SEO?
- Would voice planning help?

---

## Safe claims

Do not promise:

- guaranteed pickup
- guaranteed availability
- emergency response
- illegal/unlicensed transportation service
- insurance compliance
- exact drive times
- provider availability
- bookings
- revenue
- leads
- AI dispatch without approval
- customer messages without approval

---

## Chapter 42 field script

> For transportation and concierge, AI can help with route questions, pickup details, group requests, provider handoff, local SEO, and follow-up. The first system should collect the right details and make the next step clear without promising availability before a provider confirms.

Bridge question:

> Are you more focused on getting more route requests, organizing pickup details, or improving provider handoff?

---

## Chapter 42 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they keep transportation promises safe and realistic.

A rep fails if they imply guaranteed rides or unlicensed service.

---

# Chapter 43 — Local Brands + Retail Translation

This category includes:

- local shops
- boutiques
- makers
- pop-ups
- product brands
- neighborhood businesses
- campaign-driven local retailers
- small e-commerce/local hybrid brands

## Business reality

Local brands and retailers care about:

- product discovery
- local awareness
- promotions
- seasonal campaigns
- customer questions
- social content
- foot traffic
- repeat customers
- product education
- QR campaigns
- events/pop-ups
- email/text follow-up
- reviews
- owner time

They often need better content and customer education more than complex automation at first.

## What they actually sell

Local brands sell:

- products

- taste

- local connection

- product details

- pickup/shipping info
- store hours
- event/pop-up details

- brand story

## Where AI can create advantage

### AI-Powered Marketing

- product posts
- campaign ideas

- AI search visibility

- email/text campaign drafts
- event/pop-up content
- product education

### AI Customer Experience

Local brands can use AI for:

- product questions

## Best first offer logic

- Unclear owner → Local Brand AI Growth Review

- Event/QR interest → AI Intake + Automation

- Multiple connected needs → Full Local Brand AI Growth System

## Key discovery questions

- What products or offers need more attention?
- What questions do customers ask before buying?
- What content do you struggle to create?
- Do you use QR codes, events, pop-ups, or campaigns?
- Do customers ask about pickup, shipping, custom orders, or availability?
- What would AI help you create faster?
- What should AI never say about your products?

---

## Safe claims

Do not promise:

- guaranteed sales
- guaranteed foot traffic

- guaranteed revenue
- guaranteed rankings
- guaranteed reviews

> For local brands, AI can help with product content, promotions, customer questions, QR campaigns, events, emails, and repeat customer follow-up. The first move is usually better marketing output or a simple way to capture interest from campaigns and product questions.

Bridge question:

> Are you more focused on creating better content, answering product questions, or capturing interest from campaigns and events?

# Chapter 44 — Dashboard + Manual Translation Rules

## Chapter purpose

This chapter connects vertical translation to future dashboard logic and manual writing.

Every vertical should eventually support:

- sales scripts
- discovery questions
- objection handling
- pricing recommendations
- proposal summaries
- delivery checklists
- dashboard tags
- follow-up templates
- monthly recap logic

---

## Dashboard-ready classification

Every AI Plug lead should be classified by:

### Business type lane

- Home Services + Contractors
- Hospitality + Local Experience
- Professional Services
- Restaurants
- Transportation + Concierge
- Local Brands + Retail
- Other / Custom

### Service lane

- AI Growth Review
- AI Marketing Systems
- AI Customer Experience
- AI Intake + Automation
- AI Business Assistants
- AI Growth Management
- Full AI Growth System

### Starting point

- AI Website + Conversion System
- AI Content + Social Media Engine
- SEO + AI Search Visibility
- Paid Campaign + Landing Page System
- AI Chat Operator
- AI Phone / Voice Planning
- Smart Intake + Automation
- Internal AI Assistants + Workflow Support

### Problem type

- AI unclear / needs review
- marketing output weak
- search visibility weak
- repeated customer questions
- intake/request details messy
- follow-up inconsistent
- staff/owner busywork
- internal assistant opportunity
- owner visibility weak
- paid campaign readiness
- voice/phone planning
- multiple connected needs

### Guardrail risk

- low
- medium
- high

High-risk examples:

- professional advice
- legal/medical/tax/financial/insurance
- safety-sensitive home services
- transportation availability
- emergency response
- customer-facing AI without approved answers
- regulated claims
- unapproved public statements

---

## Manual-writing rule

Every future vertical manual must follow this rule:

> Do not simply copy the master manual. Translate the master offer into that owner’s actual daily business reality.

Each manual must include:

- what the owner sells
- what customers ask
- where AI can help first
- what staff repeats
- what marketing/content is needed
- what intake details matter
- what follow-up matters
- what safe claims apply
- what first offer makes sense
- what dashboard tags should apply

---

## Proposal-writing rule

Every vertical proposal should state:

1. The owner’s problem in plain language.
2. The first useful AI move.
3. Why that move comes first.
4. What is included.
5. What is excluded.
6. What must be approved.
7. What is not guaranteed.
8. What can expand later.

---

## Monthly recap rule

Every vertical monthly recap should include:

- what was updated
- what customer questions appeared
- what content/workflow opportunity appeared
- what needs approval
- what follow-up is due
- what AI opportunity may be next
- what should wait
- no guaranteed result framing

---

## Chapter 44 field script

Use this when handing a lead to dashboard/delivery:

> This lead should be tagged by business type, service lane, starting point, problem type, and guardrail risk. The handoff should explain the first useful AI move and what should not be built or promised yet.

Bridge question:

> What should the next person know so they do not accidentally sell or build the wrong thing?

---

## Chapter 44 training check

A rep passes this chapter if they can turn a sales conversation into vertical tags and a clean handoff.

A rep fails if the handoff only says “interested in AI.”

**AI Advantage First vertical rewrite**

`THE_AI_PLUG_MASTER_MANUAL_V3_COMPLETE_2026-05-27.md`

---

## Direction lock for this vertical

This vertical follows the corrected AI Advantage First direction.

The AI Plug is not selling “lead path” as the whole identity.

The AI Plug is not just selling a website, a form, a funnel, a chatbot, a dashboard, or a marketing campaign.

For home services and contractors, the real positioning is:

# The AI Plug helps home service businesses get an AI advantage before competitors do.

That advantage can show up through:

- better local visibility
- faster customer response
- smarter quote intake
- cleaner job/photo details
- approved homeowner answers
- internal proposal and follow-up support
- better owner visibility
- AI-assisted content and service-area marketing
- ongoing AI adaptation as tools change

The older customer-path language still matters, but it must sit underneath the bigger AI advantage.
